class="css-6n7j50"><button aria-expanded="false" aria-haspopup="true" aria-label="More sharing options" class="css-16ogagc" data-testid=""><svg class="css-uhuo44" height="13" viewbox="0 0 16 13" width="16"><path clip-rule="evenodd" d="M15.406 5.359L8.978 0v3.215C3.82 3.215.406 8.107.406 12.66 1.653 9.133 4.29 7.517 8.978 7.517v3.2l6.428-5.358z" fill="#000" fill-rule="evenodd"></path></svg></button></div></li><li class="css-60hakz"></li><li class="css-l72opv"></li></ul></div></div></li></ul></div></header></div><section class="meteredContent css-1i2y565" itemprop="articleBody" name="articleBody"><div class="css-1fanzo5 StoryBodyCompanionColumn"><div class="css-53u6y8"><p class="css-exrw3m evys1bk0">WASHINGTON — A little more than 300 years ago, the pirate Blackbeard captured a French merchant vessel, renamed it Queen Anne’s Revenge, armed it with 40 cannons and used it to pursue his calling of pillage and plunder. </p><p class="css-exrw3m evys1bk0">In November, the Supreme Court will consider a case arising from the discovery of the ship’s wreckage<!-- --> off the North Carolina coast<!-- -->. It concerns another sort of piracy: a claim that the State of North Carolina stole copyrighted videos of the submerged remains of the ship.</p><p class="css-exrw3m evys1bk0">After a private company discovered the wreckage in 1996, it hired Frederick Allen, a videographer, to film and photograph its ambitious underwater salvage work. Mr. Allen spent much of the next two decades doing that dangerous job, <!-- -->which required him to make 200 dives<!-- -->. “It’s not the same as shooting a wedding, that’s for sure,” he said of the experience.</p><p class="css-exrw3m evys1bk0">“You’re uncovering artifacts that were last touched by Blackbeard’s crew or Blackbeard himself, and it’s been 300 years since anybody has seen them,” Mr. Allen added. “It’s a chance to put your hand on the history.”</p></div><aside class="css-o6xoe7"></aside></div><div class="css-1fanzo5 StoryBodyCompanionColumn"><div class="css-53u6y8"><p class="css-exrw3m evys1bk0">Mr. Allen copyrighted the images and videos he created, but North Carolina officials ignored those copyrights <!-- -->and <!-- --> posted five of his videos on a YouTube channel and printed one of his photographs in a newsletter. In court papers, lawyers for North Carolina said Mr. Allen’s materials were public records under a state law. They added that the state was entitled to post at least portions of them for educational purposes under the “fair use” exception to copyright laws.</p><p class="css-exrw3m evys1bk0">The irony of the situation did not escape him, Mr. Allen said. “It’s modern-day piracy,” he said.</p><p class="css-exrw3m evys1bk0">He sued, winning a round in a trial court. But the federal appeals court in Richmond, Va., <a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="http://www.ca4.uscourts.gov/Opinions/171522.P.pdf" rel="noopener noreferrer" target="_blank" title="">ruled against him</a>, accepting the state’s argument that it could not be sued in federal court for copyright infringement even though Congress had enacted a law specifically authorizing such lawsuits. </p><p class="css-exrw3m evys1bk0">The Constitution, the court said, granted states sovereign immunity, shielding them from federal lawsuits. Congress, the court said, was powerless to change that.</p><p class="css-exrw3m evys1bk0">Mr. Allen found the ruling baffling. “I can sue another company,” he said. “I can sue another person. I can sue the federal government. But I can’t sue a state for a copyright infringement. That really doesn’t make any sense.”</p><p class="css-exrw3m evys1bk0">Mr. Allen has attracted powerful allies, including <a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="https://www.supremecourt.gov/DocketPDF/18/18-877/112118/20190813103611511_190805%20for%20E-Filing.pdf" rel="noopener noreferrer" target="_blank" title="">members</a> of the <a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="https://www.supremecourt.gov/DocketPDF/18/18-877/112255/20190813195203580_18877%20Amicus%20Brief%20of%20the%20Software%20%20Information%20Industry%20Association.pdf" rel="noopener noreferrer" target="_blank" title="">software</a>, <a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="https://www.supremecourt.gov/DocketPDF/18/18-877/112234/20190813164439150_18-877%20tsac%20RIAA.pdf" rel="noopener noreferrer" target="_blank" title="">recording</a> and <a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="https://www.supremecourt.gov/DocketPDF/18/18-877/112136/20190813120121554_18-877%20Allen%20v%20Governor%20Brief%20for%20Amicus%20Curiae%20Dow%20Jones.pdf" rel="noopener noreferrer" target="_blank" title="">publishing</a> industries. They say that allowing states to use copyrighted works like computer programs and textbooks without compensating their owners could have devastating economic consequences.</p></div><aside class="css-o6xoe7"></aside></div><div class="css-1fanzo5 StoryBodyCompanionColumn"><div class="css-53u6y8"><p class="css-exrw3m evys1bk0">“Sovereign immunity should not be a license to steal,” several copyright experts wrote in <a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="https://www.supremecourt.gov/DocketPDF/18/18-877/87438/20190207133413455_18-877acDavidNimmer.pdf" rel="noopener noreferrer" target="_blank" title="">a brief supporting Mr. Allen</a>. </p><p class="css-exrw3m evys1bk0"><a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="https://www.supremecourt.gov/DocketPDF/18/18-877/97173/20190422115845271_Allen%20v.%20Cooper%20BIO.pdf" rel="noopener noreferrer" target="_blank" title="">In response</a>, North Carolina pointed to <a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="https://www.law.cornell.edu/supremecourt/text/517/44" rel="noopener noreferrer" target="_blank" title="">a pair</a> of Supreme Court <a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="https://www.law.cornell.edu/supct/html/98-531.ZS.html" rel="noopener noreferrer" target="_blank" title="">decisions</a> from the 1990s, when the court, led by Chief Justice William H. Rehnquist, reinvigorated principles of federalism. Those decisions said that <a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="https://www.law.cornell.edu/constitution/amendmentxi" rel="noopener noreferrer" target="_blank" title="">the 11th Amendment</a> barred lawsuits against states for money in federal courts even when Congress had authorized them.</p><p class="css-exrw3m evys1bk0">In 1996, for instance, in <a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="https://www.law.cornell.edu/supremecourt/text/517/44" rel="noopener noreferrer" target="_blank" title="">Seminole Tribe v. Florida</a>, the court struck down a federal law authorizing Indian tribes to sue states over disputes concerning casinos.</p><p class="css-exrw3m evys1bk0">In dissent, Justice John Paul Stevens wrote that the ruling had taken a wrong turn. “It prevents Congress from providing a federal forum for a broad range of actions against states,” he wrote, “from those sounding in copyright and patent law, to those concerning bankruptcy, environmental law and the regulation of our vast national economy.”</p><p class="css-exrw3m evys1bk0">He turned out to be mostly right, as the court soon struck down a patent law but later said that <a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="https://www.nytimes.com/2006/01/24/us/justices-curb-states-immunity-from-suit.html?module=inline" title="">bankruptcy presented unique issues</a>.</p><p class="css-exrw3m evys1bk0">Decades later, in <a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="https://www.nytimes.com/2019/05/14/books/review/john-paul-stevens-making-of-a-justice.html?module=inline" title="">a memoir</a> published not long before <a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="https://www.nytimes.com/2019/07/16/us/john-paul-stevens-dead.html?module=inline" title="">he died in July</a>, Justice Stevens continued to criticize the Seminole Tribe decision.</p><p class="css-exrw3m evys1bk0">“In my view,” he wrote, “the court’s decision in Seminole Tribe is one of the most objectionable cases that the court decided during my tenure as a justice, both in its reasoning and in the impact of its holding on the efficient functioning of our national government.”</p></div><aside class="css-o6xoe7"></aside></div><div class="css-1fanzo5 StoryBodyCompanionColumn"><div class="css-53u6y8"><p class="css-exrw3m evys1bk0">Mr. Allen’s case concerns a 1990 federal law, the <a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="https://www.govinfo.gov/content/pkg/STATUTE-104/pdf/STATUTE-104-Pg2749.pdf#page=2" rel="noopener noreferrer" target="_blank" title="">Copyright Remedy Clarification Act</a>. That law tried to allow copyright lawsuits against states, saying that they “shall not be immune, under the 11th Amendment of the Constitution of the United States or under any other doctrine of sovereign immunity,” from such lawsuits.</p><p class="css-exrw3m evys1bk0">The law was enacted after <a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="https://www.copyright.gov/reports/copyright-liability-of-states-1988.pdf" rel="noopener noreferrer" target="_blank" title="">a report from the United States Copyright Office</a> warned of “dire financial and other repercussions” that would come from granting immunity to states in copyright cases.</p><p class="css-exrw3m evys1bk0">But lower courts have ruled that Congress is powerless to enact the law, relying on sweeping language in the Seminole Tribe case. Justice Department lawyers serving under both Republican and Democratic presidents have <a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="https://www.justice.gov/oip/foia-library/osg_530d_letters_5_5_2017/download" rel="noopener noreferrer" target="_blank" title="">refused to defend the law</a>, saying that Supreme Court precedents have made clear that it is unconstitutional.</p><p class="css-exrw3m evys1bk0">In <a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="https://www.supremecourt.gov/DocketPDF/18/18-877/111297/20190806124158930_No.%2018-877%20-%20Brief%20for%20Petitioners.pdf" rel="noopener noreferrer" target="_blank" title="">a Supreme Court brief</a>, lawyers for Mr. Allen argued that copyright cases required a different approach. The Constitution, after all, they said, <a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="https://fairuse.stanford.edu/law/us-constitution/" rel="noopener noreferrer" target="_blank" title="">specifically authorized Congress</a> “to promote the progress of science and useful arts, by securing for limited times to authors and inventors the exclusive right to their respective writings and discoveries.”</p><p class="css-exrw3m evys1bk0">The key words, they said, were “securing” and “exclusive,” neither of which would be honored if states were allowed to violate federal copyrights.</p><p class="css-exrw3m evys1bk0">Mr. Allen’s allies emphasized the practical consequences of a ruling against him.</p><p class="css-exrw3m evys1bk0">Without the 1990 law, <a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="https://www.supremecourt.gov/DocketPDF/18/18-877/112234/20190813164439150_18-877%20tsac%20RIAA.pdf" rel="noopener noreferrer" target="_blank" title="">a brief for the recording industry</a> said, “copyright holders, including recording artists, songwriters and other music owners whose livelihoods depend on licensing their works, face dramatic and irremediable infringement of their rights.”</p></div><aside class="css-o6xoe7"></aside></div><div class="css-1fanzo5 StoryBodyCompanionColumn"><div class="css-53u6y8"><p class="css-exrw3m evys1bk0">Mr. Allen, for his part, said he was hoping for a favorable ruling in his case, Allen v. Cooper, No. 18-877. “If we succeed,” he said, “an awful lot of artists, musicians, performers, writers and documentarians will have a much better way to protect their intellectual property from copyright infringement.”</p></div><aside class="css-o6xoe7"></aside></div><div class="css-g92qtk epkadsg3"><div class="css-ckpga5 epkadsg1">Another Copyright Case on the Supreme Court’s Docket</div><div class="css-15g2oxy epkadsg2"><div class="css-2b3w4o e16ij5yr6"><a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="https://www.nytimes.com/2019/05/13/us/politics/georgia-official-code-copyright.html?action=click&amp;module=RelatedLinks&amp;pgtype=Article"><div class="css-i9gxme e16ij5yr4"><div class="css-1j8dw05 e16ij5yr2">Accused of ‘Terrorism’ for Putting Legal Materials Online</div><time class="css-o15hd4 e16638kd0" datetime="2019-05-13T05:00:11-04:00">May 13, 2019</time></div><div class="css-1vm5oi9 e16ij5yr0"><img class="css-32rbo2 e16ij5yr1" src="https://static01.nyt.com/images/2019/05/12/us/13DC-BAR/13DC-BAR-threeByTwoSmallAt2X.jpg"/></div></a></div></div></div><div class="css-cryq2h epkadsg3"><div class="css-15g2oxy epkadsg2"></div></div></section><div class="bottom-of-article"><div class="css-1ubp8k9"></div><div class="css-wg1cha"><div class="css-19hdyf3 e1e7j8ap0"><div><p>Adam Liptak covers the Supreme Court and writes <a href="https://www.nytimes.com/column/sidebar">Sidebar,</a> a column on legal developments. 2 1.6 2V13c-.4-.2-.5-.5-.5-.7 0-.6.5-.8 1.3-.8h.4v6.2c-1.5.5-2.1 1.6-2.1 2.8 0 1.7 1.3 2.9 3.3 2.9 1.4 0 2.6-.2 3.8-.5 1-.2 2.3-.5 2.9-.5.8 0 1.1.4 1.1.9 0 .7-.3 1-.7 1.1v.2c1.6-.3 2.6-1.3 2.6-2.8s-1.5-2.4-3.1-2.4c-.8 0-2.5.3-3.7.5-1.4.3-2.8.5-3.2.5-.7 0-1.5-.3-1.5-1.3 0-.8.7-1.5 2.4-1.5.9 0 2 .1 3.1.4 1.2.3 2.3.6 3.3.6 1.5 0 2.8-.5 2.8-2.6V3.7l1.2-1-.2-.2zm-4.1 6.1c-.3.3-.7.6-1.2.6s-1-.3-1.2-.6V4.2l1-.7 1.4 1.3v3.8zm0 3c-.2-.2-.7-.5-1.2-.5s-1 .3-1.2.5V9c.2.2.7.5 1.2.5s1-.3 1.2-.5v2.6zm0 4.7c0 .8-.5 1.6-1.6 1.6h-.8V12c.2-.2.7-.5 1.2-.5s.9.3 1.2.5v4.3zm13.7-7.1l-3.2-2.3-4.9 2.8v6.5l-1 .8.1.2.8-.6 3.2 2.4 5-3V9.2zm-5.4 6.3V8.3l2.5 1.8v7.1l-2.5-1.7zm14.9-8.4h-.2c-.3.2-.6.4-.9.4-.4 0-.9-.2-1.1-.5h-.2l-1.7 1.9-1.7-1.9-3 2 .1.2.8-.5 1 1.1v6.3l-1.3 1 .2.2.6-.5 2.4 2 3.1-2.1-.1-.2-.9.5-1.2-1V9c.5.5 1.1 1 1.8 1 1.4.1 2.2-1.3 2.3-2.9zm12 9.6L123 19l-4.6-7 3.3-5.1h.2c.4.4 1 .8 1.7.8s1.2-.4 1.5-.8h.2c-.1 2-1.5 3.2-2.5 3.2s-1.5-.5-2.1-.8l-.3.5 5 7.4 1-.6v.1zm-11-.5l-1.3 1 .2.2.6-.5 2.2 2 3-2-.2-.2-.8.5-1-1V.8h-.1l-3.6 2.4v.2c.4.2 1 .3 1 1.5v11.3zM143 2.9c0-2-1.9-2.5-3.4-2.5v.3c.9 0 1.6.3 1.6 1 0 .4-.3 1-1.2 1-.7
